<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Atom</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Atom_(disambiguation)" title="Atom (disambiguation)">Atom (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table border="1" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0" class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:1em;" width="300">

The nucleus (upper right) in helium-4 is in reality spherically symmetric and closely resembles the electron cloud, although for more complicated nuclei this is not always the case. </td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">Components</a>:</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">Electrons</a> and a compact <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <b>atom</b> is the smallest constituent unit of ordinary <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> that has the properties of a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a>. Every <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> is composed of neutral or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionized" title="Ionized">ionized</a> atoms. Atoms are extremely small; typical sizes are around 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">picometers</a> (a ten-billionth of a meter, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_scale" title="Short scale">short scale</a>).
</p><p>Atoms <a href="/wiki/Matter_wave#The_de_Broglie_hypothesis" title="Matter wave">are small enough</a> that attempting to predict their behavior using classical physics – as if they were <a href="/wiki/Billiard_ball" title="Billiard ball">billiard balls</a>, for example – gives noticeably incorrect predictions due to <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum effects</a>. Through the development of physics, atomic models have incorporated <a href="#Further_developments_in_quantum_physics">quantum principles</a> to better explain and predict this behavior.
</p><p>Every atom is composed of a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> and one or more <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> bound to the nucleus. The nucleus is made of one or more <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and typically a similar number of <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>. Protons and neutrons are called <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>. More than 99.94% of an atom's <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is in the nucleus. The protons have a positive <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a>, the electrons have a negative electric charge, and the neutrons have no electric charge. If the number of protons and electrons are equal, that atom is electrically neutral. If an atom has more or fewer electrons than protons, then it has an overall negative or positive charge, respectively, and it is called an <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>.
</p><p>The electrons of an atom are attracted to the protons in an atomic nucleus by this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>. The protons and neutrons in the nucleus are attracted to each other by a different force, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a>, which is usually stronger than the electromagnetic force repelling the positively charged protons from one another. Under certain circumstances, the repelling electromagnetic force becomes stronger than the nuclear force, and nucleons can be ejected from the nucleus, leaving behind a different element: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> resulting in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">nuclear transmutation</a>.
</p><p>The number of protons in the nucleus defines to what <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> the atom belongs: for example, all <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> atoms contain 29 protons. The number of neutrons defines the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of the element. The number of electrons influences the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a> properties of an atom. Atoms can attach to one or more other atoms by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>. The ability of atoms to associate and dissociate is responsible for most of the physical changes observed in nature and is the subject of the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_atomic_theory">History of atomic theory</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_theory" title="Atomic theory">Atomic theory</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atoms_in_philosophy">Atoms in philosophy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">Atomism</a></div>
<p>The idea that matter is made up of discrete units is a very old idea, appearing in many ancient cultures such as Greece and India. The word "atom" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CF%84%CE%BF%CE%BC%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ἄτομος">ἄτομος</a></span>; <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">atomos</i></i>), meaning "uncuttable", was coined by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophers</a> <a href="/wiki/Leucippus" title="Leucippus">Leucippus</a> and his pupil <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 460 – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 370 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenLefebvre2017_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-CohenLefebvre2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Democritus taught that atoms were infinite in number, uncreated, and eternal, and that the qualities of an object result from the kind of atoms that compose it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny2004_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenLefebvre2017_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-CohenLefebvre2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Democritus's atomism was refined and elaborated by the later philosopher <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> (341 – 270 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenLefebvre2017_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-CohenLefebvre2017-4">[4]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, atomism was mostly forgotten in western Europe, but survived among some groups of Islamic philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup> During the twelfth century, atomism became known again in western Europe through references to it in the newly-rediscovered writings of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the fourteenth century, the rediscovery of major works describing atomist teachings, including <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_rerum_natura" title="De rerum natura">De rerum natura</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Laërtius">Diogenes Laërtius</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_and_Opinions_of_Eminent_Philosophers" title="Lives and Opinions of Eminent Philosophers">Lives and Opinions of Eminent Philosophers</a></i>, led to increased scholarly attention on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup> Nonetheless, because atomism was associated with the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a>, which contradicted orthodox Christian teachings, belief in atoms was not considered acceptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup> The French Catholic priest <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Gassendi" title="Pierre Gassendi">Pierre Gassendi</a> (1592 – 1655) revived Epicurean atomism with modifications, arguing that atoms were created by God and, though extremely numerous, are not infinite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CohenLefebvre2017_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-CohenLefebvre2017-4">[4]</a></sup> Gassendi's modified theory of atoms was popularized in France by the physician <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Bernier" title="François Bernier">François Bernier</a> (1620 – 1688) and in England by the natural philosopher <a href="/wiki/Walter_Charleton" title="Walter Charleton">Walter Charleton</a> (1619 – 1707).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup> The chemist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> (1627 – 1691) and the physicist <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1642 – 1727) both defended atomism and, by the end of the seventeenth century, it had become accepted by portions of the scientific community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis2010_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis2010-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_evidence-based_theory">First evidence-based theory</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Daltons_symbols.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="1067" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Daltons_symbols.gif/220px-Daltons_symbols.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Daltons_symbols.gif/330px-Daltons_symbols.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Daltons_symbols.gif/440px-Daltons_symbols.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Daltons_symbols.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various atoms and molecules as depicted in <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a>'s <i>A New System of Chemical Philosophy</i> (1808).</div></div></div>
<p>In the early 1800s, <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a> used the concept of atoms to explain why <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> always react in ratios of small whole numbers (the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_multiple_proportions" title="Law of multiple proportions">law of multiple proportions</a>). For instance, there are two types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_oxide_(disambiguation)" title="Tin oxide (disambiguation)">tin oxide</a>: one is 88.1% tin and 11.9% oxygen and the other is 78.7% tin and 21.3% oxygen (<a href="/wiki/Tin(II)_oxide" title="Tin(II) oxide">tin(II) oxide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tin_dioxide" title="Tin dioxide">tin dioxide</a> respectively). This means that 100g of tin will combine either with 13.5g or 27g of oxygen. 13.5 and 27 form a ratio of 1:2, a ratio of small whole numbers. This common pattern in chemistry suggested to Dalton that elements react in multiples of discrete units — in other words, atoms. In the case of tin oxides, one tin atom will combine with either one or two oxygen atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-From_AtomosToAtom_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-From_AtomosToAtom-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalton also believed atomic theory could explain why water absorbs different gases in different proportions. For example, he found that water absorbs <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> far better than it absorbs <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalton_1803_paper_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dalton_1803_paper-6">[6]</a></sup> Dalton hypothesized this was due to the differences between the masses and configurations of the gases' respective particles, and carbon dioxide molecules (CO<sub>2</sub>) are heavier and larger than nitrogen molecules (N<sub>2</sub>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brownian_motion">Brownian motion</span></h3>
<p>In 1827, <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botanist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Brown_(Scottish_botanist_from_Montrose)" title="Robert Brown (Scottish botanist from Montrose)">Robert Brown</a> used a microscope to look at dust grains floating in water and discovered that they moved about erratically, a phenomenon that became known as "<a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a>". This was thought to be caused by water molecules knocking the grains about. In 1905, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> proved the reality of these molecules and their motions by producing the first <a href="/wiki/Statistical_physics" title="Statistical physics">statistical physics</a> analysis of <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adp322_8_549_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-adp322_8_549-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_hoon1995_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-lee_hoon1995-9">[9]</a></sup> French physicist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Perrin" title="Jean Perrin">Jean Perrin</a> used Einstein's work to experimentally determine the mass and dimensions of atoms, thereby conclusively verifying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dalton%27s_atomic_theory" title="Dalton's atomic theory">Dalton's atomic theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e31_2_50_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-e31_2_50-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_the_electron">Discovery of the electron</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="504" height="391" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg/220px-Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg/330px-Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg/440px-Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_foil_experiment_conclusions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <b><a href="/wiki/Geiger%E2%80%93Marsden_experiment" title="Geiger–Marsden experiment">Geiger–Marsden experiment</a></b><br/> <i>Top:</i> Expected results: alpha particles passing through the plum pudding model of the atom with negligible deflection.<br/> <i>Bottom:</i> Observed results: a small portion of the particles were deflected by the concentrated positive charge of the nucleus.</div></div></div>
<p>The physicist <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> measured the mass of <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray" title="Cathode ray">cathode rays</a>, showing they were made of particles, but were around 1800 times lighter than the lightest atom, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. Therefore, they were not atoms, but a new particle, the first <i><a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic</a></i> particle to be discovered, which he originally called "<i><a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Corpuscle" title="Corpuscle">corpuscle</a></i>" but was later named <i>electron</i>, after particles postulated by <a href="/wiki/George_Johnstone_Stoney" title="George Johnstone Stoney">George Johnstone Stoney</a> in 1874. He also showed they were identical to particles given off by <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric</a> and radioactive materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomson_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomson-11">[11]</a></sup> It was quickly recognized that they are the particles that carry <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric currents</a> in metal wires, and carry the negative electric charge within atoms. Thomson was given the 1906 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> for this work. Thus he overturned the belief that atoms are the indivisible, ultimate particles of matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobel1096_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobel1096-12">[12]</a></sup> Thomson also incorrectly postulated that the low mass, negatively charged electrons were distributed throughout the atom in a uniform sea of positive charge. This became known as the <a href="/wiki/Plum_pudding_model" title="Plum pudding model">plum pudding model</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_the_nucleus">Discovery of the nucleus</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geiger-Marsden_experiment" title="Geiger-Marsden experiment">Geiger-Marsden experiment</a></div>
<p>In 1909, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Geiger" title="Hans Geiger">Hans Geiger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Marsden" title="Ernest Marsden">Ernest Marsden</a>, under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>, bombarded a metal foil with <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> to observe how they scattered. They expected all the alpha particles to pass straight through with little deflection, because Thomson's model said that the charges in the atom are so diffuse that their electric fields could not affect the alpha particles much. However, Geiger and Marsden spotted alpha particles being deflected by angles greater than 90°, which was supposed to be impossible according to Thomson's model. To explain this, Rutherford proposed that the positive charge of the atom is concentrated in a tiny nucleus at the center of the atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm21_669_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-pm21_669-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_isotopes">Discovery of isotopes</span></h3>
<p>While experimenting with the products of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>, in 1913 <a href="/wiki/Radiochemistry" title="Radiochemistry">radiochemist</a> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Soddy" title="Frederick Soddy">Frederick Soddy</a> discovered that there appeared to be more than one type of atom at each position on the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npc1921_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-npc1921-14">[14]</a></sup> The term <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> was coined by <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Todd_(doctor)" title="Margaret Todd (doctor)">Margaret Todd</a> as a suitable name for different atoms that belong to the same element. J. J. Thomson created a technique for <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">isotope separation</a> through his work on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionized_gas" title="Ionized gas">ionized gases</a>, which subsequently led to the discovery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable isotopes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prsA_89_1_1913_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-prsA_89_1_1913-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bohr_model">Bohr model</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bohr_atom_animation_2.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="275" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Bohr_atom_animation_2.gif/220px-Bohr_atom_animation_2.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Bohr_atom_animation_2.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bohr_atom_animation_2.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bohr model of the atom, with an electron making instantaneous "quantum leaps" from one orbit to another. This model is obsolete.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a></div>
<p>In 1913 the physicist <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> proposed a model in which the electrons of an atom were assumed to orbit the nucleus but could only do so in a finite set of orbits, and could jump between these orbits only in discrete changes of energy corresponding to absorption or radiation of a photon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern20050516_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern20050516-16">[16]</a></sup> This quantization was used to explain why the electrons orbits are stable (given that normally, charges in acceleration, including circular motion, lose kinetic energy which is emitted as electromagnetic radiation, see <i><a href="/wiki/Synchrotron_radiation" title="Synchrotron radiation">synchrotron radiation</a></i>) and why elements absorb and emit electromagnetic radiation in discrete spectra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bohr19221211_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-bohr19221211-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in the same year <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> provided additional experimental evidence in favor of <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Niels Bohr's theory</a>. These results refined <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>'s and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonius_Van_den_Broek" title="Antonius Van den Broek">Antonius Van den Broek</a>'s model, which proposed that the atom contains in its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> a number of positive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_charge" title="Nuclear charge">nuclear charges</a> that is equal to its (atomic) number in the periodic table. Until these experiments, <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> was not known to be a physical and experimental quantity. That it is equal to the atomic nuclear charge remains the accepted atomic model today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pais-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_bonding_explained">Chemical bonding explained</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">Chemical bonds</a> between atoms were now explained, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilbert_Newton_Lewis" title="Gilbert Newton Lewis">Gilbert Newton Lewis</a> in 1916, as the interactions between their constituent electrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacs38_4_762_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacs38_4_762-19">[19]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical properties</a> of the elements were known to largely repeat themselves according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_law" title="Periodic law">periodic law</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> in 1919 the American chemist <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a> suggested that this could be explained if the electrons in an atom were connected or clustered in some manner. Groups of electrons were thought to occupy a set of <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">electron shells</a> about the nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacs41_6_868_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacs41_6_868-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_developments_in_quantum_physics">Further developments in quantum physics</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stern%E2%80%93Gerlach_experiment" title="Stern–Gerlach experiment">Stern–Gerlach experiment</a> of 1922 provided further evidence of the quantum nature of atomic properties. When a beam of silver atoms was passed through a specially shaped magnetic field, the beam was split in a way correlated with the direction of an atom's angular momentum, or <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>. As this spin direction is initially random, the beam would be expected to deflect in a random direction. Instead, the beam was split into two directional components, corresponding to the atomic <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> being oriented up or down with respect to the magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fop17_6_575_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-fop17_6_575-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1925 <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a> published the first consistent mathematical formulation of quantum mechanics (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrix_Mechanics" title="Matrix Mechanics">Matrix Mechanics</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pais-18">[18]</a></sup> One year earlier, in 1924, <a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Broglie" title="Louis de Broglie">Louis de Broglie</a> had proposed that all particles behave to an extent like waves and, in 1926, <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger" title="Erwin Schrödinger">Erwin Schrödinger</a> used this idea to develop a mathematical model of the atom (Wave Mechanics) that described the electrons as three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">waveforms</a> rather than point particles.
</p><p>A consequence of using waveforms to describe particles is that it is mathematically impossible to obtain precise values for both the <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">position</a> and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> of a particle at a given point in time; this became known as the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>, formulated by <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a> in 1927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pais-18">[18]</a></sup> In this concept, for a given accuracy in measuring a position one could only obtain a range of probable values for momentum, and vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
This model was able to explain observations of atomic behavior that previous models could not, such as certain structural and <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">spectral</a> patterns of atoms larger than hydrogen. Thus, the planetary model of the atom was discarded in favor of one that described <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbital</a> zones around the nucleus where a given electron is most likely to be observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown2007_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown2007-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harrison2000_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-harrison2000-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_of_the_neutron">Discovery of the neutron</span></h3>
<p>The development of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometer</a> allowed the mass of atoms to be measured with increased accuracy. The device uses a magnet to bend the trajectory of a beam of ions, and the amount of deflection is determined by the ratio of an atom's mass to its charge. The chemist <a href="/wiki/Francis_William_Aston" title="Francis William Aston">Francis William Aston</a> used this instrument to show that isotopes had different masses. The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> of these isotopes varied by integer amounts, called the <a href="/wiki/Whole_number_rule" title="Whole number rule">whole number rule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm39_6_449_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-pm39_6_449-26">[26]</a></sup> The explanation for these different isotopes awaited the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>, an uncharged particle with a mass similar to the <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>, by the physicist <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> in 1932. Isotopes were then explained as elements with the same number of protons, but different numbers of neutrons within the nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chadwick1935_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-chadwick1935-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Fission.2C_high-energy_physics_and_condensed_matter"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission,_high-energy_physics_and_condensed_matter">Fission, high-energy physics and condensed matter</span></h3>
<p>In 1938, the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a>, a student of Rutherford, directed neutrons onto uranium atoms expecting to get <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranium elements</a>. Instead, his chemical experiments showed <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a> as a product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowden_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHF_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHF-29">[29]</a></sup> A year later, <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> and her nephew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_Frisch" title="Otto Frisch">Otto Frisch</a> verified that Hahn's result were the first experimental <i>nuclear fission</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature143_3615_239_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature143_3615_239-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroeder_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-schroeder-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1944, Hahn received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_prize" title="Nobel prize">Nobel prize</a> in chemistry. Despite Hahn's efforts, the contributions of Meitner and Frisch were not recognized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pt50_9_26_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-pt50_9_26-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1950s, the development of improved <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Particle_detector" title="Particle detector">particle detectors</a> allowed scientists to study the impacts of atoms moving at high energies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kullander2001_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-kullander2001-33">[33]</a></sup> Neutrons and protons were found to be <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>, or composites of smaller particles called <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_model_of_particle_physics" title="Standard model of particle physics">standard model of particle physics</a> was developed that so far has successfully explained the properties of the nucleus in terms of these sub-atomic particles and the forces that govern their interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npp1990_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-npp1990-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subatomic_particles">Subatomic particles</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">Subatomic particle</a></div>
<p>Though the word <i>atom</i> originally denoted a particle that cannot be cut into smaller particles, in modern scientific usage the atom is composed of various <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>. The constituent particles of an atom are the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>; all three are <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen-1</a> atom has no neutrons and the <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">hydron ion</a> has no electrons.
</p><p>The electron is by far the least massive of these particles at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6969911000000000000♠</span>9.11<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−31</sup> kg</span>, with a negative <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electrical charge</a> and a size that is too small to be measured using available techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> It was the lightest particle with a positive rest mass measured, until the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a> mass. Under ordinary conditions, electrons are bound to the positively charged nucleus by the attraction created from opposite electric charges. If an atom has more or fewer electrons than its atomic number, then it becomes respectively negatively or positively charged as a whole; a charged atom is called an <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>. Electrons have been known since the late 19th century, mostly thanks to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.J._Thomson" title="J.J. Thomson">J.J. Thomson</a>; see <a href="/wiki/History_of_subatomic_physics" title="History of subatomic physics">history of subatomic physics</a> for details.
</p><p>Protons have a positive charge and a mass 1,836 times that of the electron, at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6973167260000000000♠</span>1.6726<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−27</sup> kg</span>. The number of protons in an atom is called its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> (1919) observed that nitrogen under alpha-particle bombardment ejects what appeared to be hydrogen nuclei. By 1920 he had accepted that the hydrogen nucleus is a distinct particle within the atom and named it <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>.
</p><p>Neutrons have no electrical charge and have a free mass of 1,839 times the mass of the electron,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6973169290000000000♠</span>1.6929<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−27</sup> kg</span>, the heaviest of the three constituent particles, but it can be reduced by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a>. Neutrons and protons (collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>) have comparable dimensions—on the order of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6985250000000000000♠</span>2.5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−15</sup> m</span>—although the 'surface' of these particles is not sharply defined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The neutron was discovered in 1932 by the English physicist <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of physics, electrons are truly elementary particles with no internal structure. However, both protons and neutrons are composite particles composed of <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a> called <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>. There are two types of quarks in atoms, each having a fractional electric charge. Protons are composed of two <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up quarks</a> (each with charge +<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">2</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">3</span></span>) and one <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quark</a> (with a charge of −<span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">3</span></span>). Neutrons consist of one up quark and two down quarks. This distinction accounts for the difference in mass and charge between the two particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pdg2002_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pdg2002-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schombert2006_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-schombert2006-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The quarks are held together by the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interaction</a> (or strong force), which is mediated by <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a>. The protons and neutrons, in turn, are held to each other in the nucleus by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a>, which is a residuum of the strong force that has somewhat different range-properties (see the article on the nuclear force for more). The gluon is a member of the family of <a href="/wiki/Gauge_boson" title="Gauge boson">gauge bosons</a>, which are elementary particles that mediate physical forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pdg2002_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-pdg2002-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schombert2006_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-schombert2006-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleus">Nucleus</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">Atomic nucleus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="671" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/220px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/330px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/440px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> needed for a nucleon to escape the nucleus, for various isotopes</div></div></div>
<p>All the bound protons and neutrons in an atom make up a tiny <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, and are collectively called <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>. The radius of a nucleus is approximately equal to 1.07 <span class="nowrap"><sup style="margin-right: -0.5em; vertical-align: 0.8em;">3</sup>√<span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;"><i>A</i></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a>, where <i>A</i> is the total number of nucleons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This is much smaller than the radius of the atom, which is on the order of 10<sup>5</sup> fm. The nucleons are bound together by a short-ranged attractive potential called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Residual_strong_force" title="Residual strong force">residual strong force</a>. At distances smaller than 2.5 fm this force is much more powerful than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">electrostatic force</a> that causes positively charged protons to repel each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atoms of the same <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> have the same number of protons, called the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>. Within a single element, the number of neutrons may vary, determining the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of that element. The total number of protons and neutrons determine the <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>. The number of neutrons relative to the protons determines the stability of the nucleus, with certain isotopes undergoing <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wenner2007_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-wenner2007-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The proton, the electron, and the neutron are classified as <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>. Fermions obey the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a> which prohibits <i><a href="/wiki/Identical_particles" title="Identical particles">identical</a></i> fermions, such as multiple protons, from occupying the same quantum state at the same time. Thus, every proton in the nucleus must occupy a quantum state different from all other protons, and the same applies to all neutrons of the nucleus and to all electrons of the electron cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raymond_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-raymond-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>A nucleus that has a different number of protons than neutrons can potentially drop to a lower energy state through a radioactive decay that causes the number of protons and neutrons to more closely match. As a result, atoms with matching numbers of protons and neutrons are more stable against decay. However, with increasing atomic number, the mutual repulsion of the protons requires an increasing proportion of neutrons to maintain the stability of the nucleus, which slightly modifies this trend of equal numbers of protons to neutrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raymond_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-raymond-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="416" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg/170px-Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg/255px-Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg/340px-Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wpdms_physics_proton_proton_chain_1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of a nuclear fusion process that forms a deuterium nucleus, consisting of a proton and a neutron, from two protons. A <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> (e<sup>+</sup>)—an <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> electron—is emitted along with an electron <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The number of protons and neutrons in the atomic nucleus can be modified, although this can require very high energies because of the strong force. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> occurs when multiple atomic particles join to form a heavier nucleus, such as through the energetic collision of two nuclei. For example, at the core of the Sun protons require energies of 3–10 keV to overcome their mutual repulsion—the <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_barrier" title="Coulomb barrier">coulomb barrier</a>—and fuse together into a single nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mihos2002_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-mihos2002-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">Nuclear fission</a> is the opposite process, causing a nucleus to split into two smaller nuclei—usually through radioactive decay. The nucleus can also be modified through bombardment by high energy subatomic particles or photons. If this modifies the number of protons in a nucleus, the atom changes to a different chemical element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lbnl20070330_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-lbnl20070330-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-makhijani_saleska2001_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-makhijani_saleska2001-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>If the mass of the nucleus following a fusion reaction is less than the sum of the masses of the separate particles, then the difference between these two values can be emitted as a type of usable energy (such as a <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>, or the kinetic energy of a <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a>), as described by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">mass–energy equivalence</a> formula, <i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup>, where <i>m</i> is the mass loss and <i>c</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. This deficit is part of the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> of the new nucleus, and it is the non-recoverable loss of the energy that causes the fused particles to remain together in a state that requires this energy to separate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fusion of two nuclei that create larger nuclei with lower atomic numbers than <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>—a total nucleon number of about 60—is usually an <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic process</a> that releases more energy than is required to bring them together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajp63_7_653_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ajp63_7_653-48">[48]</a></sup> It is this energy-releasing process that makes nuclear fusion in <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> a self-sustaining reaction. For heavier nuclei, the binding energy per <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a> in the nucleus begins to decrease. That means fusion processes producing nuclei that have atomic numbers higher than about 26, and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic masses</a> higher than about 60, is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic_reaction" title="Endothermic reaction">endothermic process</a>. These more massive nuclei can not undergo an energy-producing fusion reaction that can sustain the <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a> of a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raymond_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-raymond-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_cloud">Electron cloud</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">Atomic orbital</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">Electron configuration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potential_energy_well.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="535" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Potential_energy_well.svg/220px-Potential_energy_well.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Potential_energy_well.svg/330px-Potential_energy_well.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Potential_energy_well.svg/440px-Potential_energy_well.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potential_energy_well.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A potential well, showing, according to <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>, the minimum energy <i>V</i>(<i>x</i>) needed to reach each position <i>x</i>. Classically, a particle with energy <i>E</i> is constrained to a range of positions between <i>x</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>x</i><sub>2</sub>.</div></div></div>
<p>The electrons in an atom are attracted to the protons in the nucleus by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>. This force binds the electrons inside an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic" title="Electrostatic">electrostatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Potential_well" title="Potential well">potential well</a> surrounding the smaller nucleus, which means that an external source of energy is needed for the electron to escape. The closer an electron is to the nucleus, the greater the attractive force. Hence electrons bound near the center of the potential well require more energy to escape than those at greater separations.
</p><p>Electrons, like other particles, have properties of both a <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave–particle duality">particle and a wave</a>. The electron cloud is a region inside the potential well where each electron forms a type of three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a>—a wave form that does not move relative to the nucleus. This behavior is defined by an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbital</a>, a mathematical function that characterises the probability that an electron appears to be at a particular location when its position is measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science157_3784_13_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-science157_3784_13-49">[49]</a></sup> Only a discrete (or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/quantize" title="wikt:quantize">quantized</a>) set of these orbitals exist around the nucleus, as other possible wave patterns rapidly decay into a more stable form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brucat2008_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brucat2008-50">[50]</a></sup> Orbitals can have one or more ring or node structures, and differ from each other in size, shape and orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manthey2001_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-manthey2001-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S-p-Orbitals.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1813" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/S-p-Orbitals.svg/220px-S-p-Orbitals.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/S-p-Orbitals.svg/330px-S-p-Orbitals.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/S-p-Orbitals.svg/440px-S-p-Orbitals.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S-p-Orbitals.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wave functions of the first five atomic orbitals. The three 2p orbitals each display a single angular <a href="/wiki/Node_(physics)" title="Node (physics)">node</a> that has an orientation and a minimum at the center.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Atomic_orbitals_and_periodic_table_construction.ogv/854px--Atomic_orbitals_and_periodic_table_construction.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="64" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Atomic_orbitals_and_periodic_table_construction.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/61/Atomic_orbitals_and_periodic_table_construction.ogv/Atomic_orbitals_and_periodic_table_construction.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm;
